Chapter 1
Introduction
OMX Copenhagen 20 is the Copenhagen Stock ExchangeŌĆÖs leading share index.
The index consists of the 20 most actively traded shares on the Copenhagen
Stock Exchange. The limited number of constituents guarantees that all the
underlying shares of the index have excellent liquidity. The composition of the
OMXC20 index is revised twice a year.
All shares listed on NASDAQ Copenhagen are ranked according to free ’¼éoat
adjusted market capitalization. The top 25 shares constitute the OMXC20 ba-
sic portfolio.
The 20 shares with highest turnover in the OMXC20 basic portfolio will be in-
cluded in the new OMXC20 portfolio. This portfolio is also called the OMXC20
active portfolio.
This means that large companies like Danske Bank , Novo
Nordisk and AP M├Ėller - M├”rsk make up a very large part of the index, so
changes in their courses have a much greater impact on the index than changes
in the price of smaller companies, such as DSV.[11]
OMXC20 is a market index in line with, for example, the US S&P 500 Index
and re’¼éects developments in the total market value of listed companies.
==PAGE:14==
2
Introduction
1.1
Project plan
The price of shares for the C20 group of stocks on the Danish Stock Exchange
is updated daily. Based on data for share prices on all 20 shares, and exogenous
variables such as exchange rates and commodity prices, the aim of the project
is to model the share prices with a multivariate times series model, and formu-
late an investment model based on predictions of the multivariate time series
model.The model must be evaluated through simulation and the potential gain
must be quanti’¼üed. The student selects and obtain data for exogenous variables,
under supervision.
Here is shown the project plan made in the end of March
==PAGE:15==
1.1 Project plan
3

WEEK 8-12/03 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Chapter 1 
Literature Studies 
Find information about the C20 and the 
companies inside it. 
Understand which models can be used for given 
the data. 
Understanding and Preprocessing of the 
dataset 
Deal with irregularly spaced time series problem, 
by assuming a business calendar which goes from 
Monday to Friday. Decide how to deal with 
weekends and how to impute data for the 
holidays that fall during the business week. 
 
Chapter 2 
Univariate Analysis 
Differentiate the share prices time series in order 
to have them stationary in mean 
Find which order of ARMA model (including 
seasonality) is more appropriate for the 
differenced price series. 
Consider which exogenous variables can affect 
the stock returns in order to have a more precise 
model. Introduce dummies to model season and 
Monday effect. 
Operates Selection of the exogenous variable 
using linear regression models. 
Combine linear models and ARMA in order to fit 
a regression with ARIMA errors model. Do a 
model selection on the results. 
Include lagged exogenous variables in the model.

4
Introduction

Estimate the values of the returns during the 
holidays, by predicting their values based on the 
past value given by the selected model and re-
estimate the models. 
 
Chapter 3 
Multivariate Analysis 
Understanding of the multivariate framework, 
studying of CCF and pre-whitening processes. 
Use marima in order to fit multivariate ARMA 
and ARMAX models of groups of shares 
belonging to the same sector. 
Include seasonality and exogenous variables. 
Try to build a unique model that includes al the 
selected covariates and exogenous variable with 
the aid of the marima function. 
 
 
Chapter 4 
Investment Analysis 
Using the model/models built during the previous 
week in order to forecast future values of prices 
and returns of the C20ŌĆÖs.  
Built and analyze the constrained optimization 
problem of maximizing the profit with various 
constraints namely the transactions cost, the 
presence of a budget or the willingness of the 
investor for risk. 
Trying to construct possible investment plan. 
Compare the predicted values with the real ones. 
 
Conclusion 
Summarize the work done. Finalize the thesis 
report. 
Preparation for the defense of the thesis.

1.3 General Time Series Framework
9
Figure 1.3: Companies logos 3
1.3
General Time Series Framework
A time series is an ordered sequence of observations, the ordering is usually
through time,but could be taken through other dimensions as well. Time se-
ries occur in a variety of ’¼üelds.
In agriculture, we industry production and
prices. In business and economics, we observe daily closing stock prices, weekly
interest rates, monthly price indices, quarterly sales, and yearly earnings. In
engineering, we observe sound, electric signals, and voltage. In transportation ,
we record the number of passenger travelling on a certain route over the years.
In medical studies, we measure electroencephalogram (EEG) and electrocardio-
gram (EKG) tracings. In meteorology, we observe hourly wind speeds, daily
temperature, and annual rainfall.The list of areas in which time series is ob-
served and studied is endless.[19]
The intrinsic nature of a time series is that its observations are dependent or
correlated, and the order of the observations is therefore important.
Hence,
statistical procedures and techniques that rely on independence assumption are
no longer applicable, and di’¼Ćerent methods are needed.
A time series, such as interest rates, share prices of a stock, and volume of
trades, which are taken only at speci’¼üc time intervals, is said to be discrete. In
this elaborate, we deal exclusively with discrete time series.
We aim our analysis to a better understanding and description of the selected
time series, the forecasting of future values, and the examination of interrelation-
ships among several related time series variables of interest and determination of
appropriate multivariate dynamic models to represent these joint relationships
among the variables over time.
1.3.1
Operators
Some operator are used many times in the time series modeling framework.[4]
The backward shift operator B(sometimes called lag operator L), is de’¼üned by

==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2
Univariate Analysis
For each stock in the C20, we tried to built an univariate model of the closing
price considering at ’¼ürst an ARIMA model and then including some exogenous
predictors,in order to have an ARIMAX model.
For each of the twenty stock of the index the model selecting procedure was the
same but the obtained results were di’¼Ćerent.
2.1
Pre-processing
The Danish stock market is open for about 261 days during the year,each week
from Monday to Friday excluding the holidays.The holidays are ten days each
year, but they could also fall during the weekend.
In our analysis we decided to consider a business calendar which goes from
Monday to Friday and includes the holidays, in order to have regular time se-
ries.Therefore each times series has a period of 5 days.
The closing prices during the holiday were initially set as the same price of the
day before the holidays,then they have been re-estimated using a one step ahead
forecast from the selected model.
==PAGE:26==
14
Univariate Analysis
2.2
Backward selection
The process consist in is ’¼ütting a model M, measuring the signi’¼ücance of its
coe’¼ācient,selecting those coe’¼ācient which have a p-value above a de’¼üned sig-
ni’¼ücance level ╬▒ and eliminate the less signi’¼ücant among the selected ones.A
new model,obtained my eliminating the less signi’¼ücant coe’¼ācient,is ’¼ütted to
the data.
The process is repeated until all the coe’¼ācients in the model are
signi’¼ücant.
On way to measure the p-values of a model is the likehood ratio test. Given a
model M, its likelihood L(M) is a function of the parameters in model eval-
uated at the MLE (Maximum Likelihood Estimators). Comparing each sub-
model with the original is possible to estimate the signi’¼ücance(p-value) of the
coe’¼ācients, if M0 is the original model and Mk is the sub-model in with the
coe’¼ācient k has been removed than as a consequence of Wilks theorem [20]
x = ŌłÆ2 log L(Mk)
L(M0) Ōł╝Žć2
1
so is possible to get the p-value of the coe’¼ācient k as
p = 1 ŌłÆF(x)
where F is the cumulative distribution function.
Once the coe’¼ācients p-values have been estimated, if some are not signi’¼ücant
i.e.bigger then a level ╬▒ (usually ╬▒ = 0.05),the less signi’¼ücant is removed and a
new model (M) is obtained.
The selection steps are repeated until all the coe’¼ācient in the selected model
are signi’¼ücant(p < ╬▒).
The more statistical tests we perform, the more likely we are to reject the null
hypothesis when it is true (i.e., a ŌĆ£false alarm,ŌĆØ also called a "Type 1" error). We
tried to avoid this problem by making the ╬▒ level more stringent (i.e., smaller)
using the Bonferroni-Holm method.[1]
The Bonferroni corrected ╬▒ for C comparisons, ╬▒B,C is
╬▒B,C = ╬▒
C
In Bonferroni correction with HolmŌĆÖs approach, the corrected ╬▒ is di’¼Ćrent for
each test, so for the ith-test, is
╬▒H,i|C =
╬▒
(C ŌłÆi + 1)
This method is applied sequentially, from the lower p-value to the highest and
it stop at the ’¼ürst pi > ╬▒H,i|C

2.4
Exogenous variables
In order to have better models we decided to introduce some exogenous vari-
ables that may a’¼Ćect the share price ( and so also the return).
There are many factors that a’¼Ćect the stock market, this can be grouped by
type in fundamental factors,technical factors and market sentiment.[6]
In our analysis we include mainly technical factors we considered therefore eco-
nomic indicators, currency,commodity,related markets index,we also considered
temperature and quantity of rain as factors that may a’¼Ćect the market senti-
ment.
The economic indicators selected are Interest rate INR and Gross Domestic
Product GDP,the currency are DKKEUR, DKKUSD and the cryptocurrency
Bitcoin.
For related markets we took the index for the US stock market S&P500, the
Frankfurt Stock Exchange index DAX 30 and the index Euronext 100 as Euro-
pean representative indexes.
The are many sources that prove the relationship between some commodities
and a speci’¼üc stock market, like a study on the e’¼Ćect of copper and oil prices on
the stock price index in Zambia[10] or one on the relationship between stocks
prices and commodity indexes prices in Turkey [8]. We examined the e’¼Ćect of
gold ,oil,corn,co’¼Ćee and wheat prices, as this are some of the commodities that
a’¼Ćect the market the most [12] [5].
We decided also to include 3 dummies variables in order to model the e’¼Ćect of

0 on the other weekdays,while the variables Winter,Spring,Summer are peri-

Exogenous variables selection
In order to reduce the size of the model and select only the most signi’¼ücant

di’¼Ćerent stock prices as response variable.
Given a output y and k multiple predictors xk multiple regression model can be
expressed as
yt = ╬▓0 + ╬▓1x1,t + ╬▓2x2,t + ┬Ę ┬Ę ┬Ę + ╬▓kxk,t + ŽĄt

ing into account the e’¼Ćects of all the other predictors in the model. Thus, the

Dynamic regression model including lagged variables
Sometimes, the impact of a predictor which is included in a regression model
will not be simple and immediate,the past values of the exogenous variable may,

The dummies variable were not lagged because lagging the dummies seasons
will have no signi’¼ücant e’¼Ćect considered how theese variables were built and
lagging the Monday will move the e’¼Ćect on another week day.
Other variables for which lagged value are not signi’¼ücant are GDP,INR and
Temp_C, in fact the temperature follows almost linear patterns, the interest
rate is constant for long time periods and the GDP is constructed by linear
interpolation of quarterly data about the Danish economy (the values of the
previous days are almost the same) therefore we set ╬│8 = ╬│9 = ╬│14 = 0.
We decide to include just the ’¼ürst order lagged variables,for computational is-
sues, as the models required a long time for the estimation.Given a variable xi
the lagged variable xil1 is the value of the variable at time t-1.
A more accurate estimates of the signi’¼ücant lags can be done using a prewith-

2.5.2
Holidays values Estimation
The market is close during national holiday which fall in days from Monday to
Friday, but as we wanted to have a regular spaced time series, we decided to
estimate the values of the stock prices( and so returns) in those holiday.
We repeated the process of selecting the signi’¼ücant coe’¼ācients backward se-
lection and than re-estimating the model in order to improve the estimates of
returns during holidays and include their e’¼Ćect in the models.
At ’¼ürst the value of the returns during the holiday were set to the value of the
’¼ürst previous working day as showed in the scheme below.
Figure 2.3: Holiday Prices Estimation Scheme 1
Secondly the price in the ’¼ürst group of holidays (Easter 2013 for most of the
stocks) was estimate using a n-step ahead forecast from the dynamic regression
model (with lag 1 included) M0 selected with backard selection and applied on
the data until the day before those holidays. Than a new dynamic regression
model M0,1 with reduced coe’¼ācients was estimated using the data up to the
next holiday group and using the model M0 as starting point,and the values
during the new holiday group were predicted.The estimation-prediction process
was repeated for each holiday,then a new model M1 including all the data was
estimated.
==PAGE:37==
2.5 Dynamic regression model
25
The whole process was repeated getting new models M2, . . . , Mj until the model
Mj was the same as MjŌłÆ1
As the process was too long,we used this method only on a few stocks and we
instead used a simpler method in which the same model is ’¼ütted progressively
on the data and is used for forecasting the values in each holidays and than a
new model is estimated(i.e. the are not submodels Mi,j)
Figure 2.4: Holiday Prices Estimation Scheme 2
2.5.3
Results

Chapter 3
Multivariate Analysis
3.1
CCF and Prewhitening
A very useful tool for the identi’¼ücation of multivariate time series is the CCF
Cross-correlation function.
Given an input series and an output series, they can be seen as a realiza-
tion of a bivariate stochastic process (Xt, Yt) we assume that the di’¼Ćerenced
process(xt, yt),where xt = ŌłćdxXt and yt = ŌłćdyYt, is stationary.
Therefore xt and yt have constant means ┬Ąx and ┬Ąy and constant variances Žā2
x
and Žā2
y the cross-covariance of the process(xt, yt) at lag k is de’¼üned as
╬│xx(k) = E[(xt ŌłÆ┬Ąx)(xt+k ŌłÆ┬Ąx)] = E[(xt ŌłÆ┬Ąx)(xtŌłÆk ŌłÆ┬Ąx)]
╬│yy(k) = E[(yt ŌłÆ┬Ąy)(yt+k ŌłÆ┬Ąy)] = E[(yt ŌłÆ┬Ąx)(ytŌłÆk ŌłÆ┬Ąy)]
╬│xy(k) = E[(xt ŌłÆ┬Ąx)(yt+k ŌłÆ┬Ąy)]
╬│yx(k) = E[(yt ŌłÆ┬Ąy)(xt+k ŌłÆ┬Ąy)] = ╬│xy(ŌłÆk)
So the cross-correlation is given by
Žüxy(k) = ╬│xy(k)
ŽāxŽāy
Prewhitening a series prior to a cross-correlation analysis can be useful. Prewhite
a time series means to transform a time series order to be white noise.If this
==PAGE:42==
30
Multivariate Analysis
transformation is not applied to at least one of the two time series, there is no
simple way to tell if a cross-correlation estimate is signi’¼ücantly di’¼Ćerent from
zero.[16]
Given a stationary input series xt that follows an ARMA process
Žåx(B)xt = ╬Ėx(B)╬Ąt
the prewhitten(transformed) series can be written as
╬Ėx(B)ŌłÆ1Žåx(B)xt = ╬Ąt
The same transformation can be applied to the stationary time series yt obtain-
ing
╬Čt = ╬Ėx(B)ŌłÆ1Žåx(B)yt
3.1.1
Example
In this example we considered the relationship between historical prices of Pan-
dora and SP500.
We di’¼Ćerenced the series in order to have them stationary,the input variable xt
is the return on SP500, while the output variable yt is the return on Pandora.
From the estimation xt, follows an ARMA(4, 3)(3, 3)5 process while yt is an
ARMA(4, 4)(3, 3)5, Figure 3.1 show the time series and their respective ACF
and PACF.
The prewhitten(transformed) series can be written as
╬śx(B5)ŌłÆ1╬Ėx(B)ŌłÆ1Žåx(B)╬”x(B5)┬»xt = ╬Ąt
with
┬»xt = xt ŌłÆ1.019
Žåx(B) = 1 + 0.454B + 0.604B2 + ŌłÆ0.412B3 + 0.063B4
╬Ėx(B) = 1 + 0.451B + 0.548B2 + ŌłÆ0.456B3
╬”x(B5) = 1 + ŌłÆ0.21B5 + 0.086B10 + 0.837B15
╬śx(B5) = 1 + ŌłÆ0.245B5 + 0.104B10 + 0.832B15
The same transformation can be applied to the stationary time series yt obtain-
ing
╬Čt = ╬śx(B5)ŌłÆ1╬Ėx(B)ŌłÆ1Žåx(B)╬”x(B5)yt
The sample CCF is helpful for identifying lags of the x-variable that might be
useful predictors of yt.In R, the sample CCF is de’¼üned as the set of sample
correlations between xt+h and yt for h = 0, ┬▒1, ┬▒2, ┬▒3, . . .

3.1.2
Prewhitening on all the series
We applied the prewhitening process on all the possible combination of a share
price and an exogenous variable,excluding dummies and variable for which the
lagged values arenŌĆÖt useful to built the model(GDP,INR,Temp).
We used this process in order to select the signi’¼ücant lags of the exogenous
variables in the multivariate models.
As in the previous section from each of the CCF on prewhitten data, shown
in Appendix A.2, is possible to select the signi’¼ücant lags(negative) and than
’¼ütting a regression model for each of the stocks including all the lags up to the
maximum of the signi’¼ücant lags for each of the exogenous variables considered.
For each variable the maximum lag considered is always less or equal than 22
as we decided to consider the e’¼Ćects up to a month in the past(22 is the aver-
age length of a business month).The signi’¼ücant lag for each stock are listed in
Appendix A.3 Listing A.5

3.3 marima Models
35
3.3
marima Models
For the coe’¼ācients of the ARMA model we used the marima function written
by Henrik Spliid in R.
The function allow to add seasonality coe’¼ācients on the model, in the same
way as if they were exogenous variables. Therefore given an n-variate process
Yt = (y1,t . . . yn,t) that follows and ARMA(p,q) and a desired autocorrelation
over a relatively long time period s the new variables are generated by lagging
the old variabless-1 time steps YtŌłÆsŌłÆ1 = (y1,tŌłÆsŌłÆ1 . . . yn,tŌłÆsŌłÆ1) in this way a
new 2n-variate variable Yt is generated.
We this technique is possible to include seasonal lagging for the share prices
(s=5) and also long term lag for the exogenous variable( the maximum lag con-
sidered is 22 the average length of a business month). The model coe’¼ācients
is estimated considering the lagged variables of the AR part as pure regression
coe’¼ācients as the original series depends on the lagged variables, but we did
not consider a feedback e’¼Ćect.
Given a model estimated by marima is it possible to select only the signi’¼ücant
coe’¼ācient by using a backward selection process.
The coe’¼ācient are estimated using a pseudo-regression model, the function re-
turns also the F-statistic and the relative p-value for each of the coe’¼ācients,which
can be used for the backward selection.
In the multivariate case there is not a unique way to perform a backward selec-
tion as we a matrix of coe’¼ācient for the di’¼Ćerent AR and MA orders.
All the models considered are in the form (3.5) where the exogenous term X
can also include the seasonal lagged Y variables or long period lagged X vari-
ables. For a model in this form,where Y is a r variate time series while X is
in m = n ŌłÆr dimensions, an AR matrix and an MA matrix of coe’¼ācients will
have the form
Žå =

(3.7)
There fore the non zero part of the autoregressive and moving average matrix
are, respectively two r ├Ś n matrices.
Both matrices has a sparse structure with a lot of zero coe’¼ācients, the MA ma-
trix will only have r2 non zero coe’¼ācients or 2r2 if a seasonal MA component
is also considered.
Given a model of two stock prices and an exogenous variable without seasonal
lags(as the one in Section 3.3.1) starting from an ARMA(3,3) model there will
==PAGE:48==
36
Multivariate Analysis
be three AR matrices 2 ├Ś 3 and three MA matrices 2 ├Ś 2 of non-zero coe’¼ācient
which is possible to test for signi’¼ücance.
The selection procedure that we decided to adopt is similar to the procedure
used in univariate analysis, we in fact considered the vector formed by all the
p-values of the coe’¼ācients and if there is at least one non signi’¼ücant coe’¼ācient(
with p-value>0.05) removed the less signi’¼ücant one among the non signi’¼ücant
coe’¼ācients and re estimated the model, the process continued until there are
only signi’¼ücant coe’¼ācients.As in the univariate model we applied an HolmŌĆÖs
correction in order to avoid false rejection of the statistical test.
Another possible approach would be to consider only the coe’¼ācient of the max-
imal AR and MA order matrices and then proceed to an elimination-estimation
process until there are only signi’¼ücant coe’¼ācients in those two matrices or if
there is not any eliminate the matrix and therefore reduce the order of the
model.Then repeat this step until all the coe’¼ācients appear to be signi’¼ücant.
A third possible approach is to check if the coe’¼ācient of a matrix are all zero
using statistical test.
With an F-test with null hypothesis H0 : a1 = a2 = ┬Ę ┬Ę ┬Ę = aq = 0 ,where
a1, a2, . . . , aq are the non-zero matrix coe’¼ācients, the test statistic can be writ-
ten as:
F0 = (n ŌłÆ1)(SSRr ŌłÆSSR0)
qSSR0
where SSRr stands for the sum of the squared residuals of the restricted model,SSR0
is the same for the unrestricted model, n is the number of observations and q is
the number of restrictions (or the number of coe’¼ācients being jointly tested)

3.4.1
Model Coe’¼ācients
In order to consider the e’¼Ćect of lagged exogenous variables, for each group we
calculate the signi’¼ücant lags by taking all the lags selected by the linear models
of the shares in the group obtained after prewhitening as described before( Ap-
pendix A.3 Listing A.5).
The prewhitening process was done for each pairs formed by a share returns
and transformed exogenous variable (so that each of them is stationary) using
the linear regression models.
For each exogenous variable we considered all the lag up to the maximal signif-
icant one inside the shares group.
Given an exogenous variable x, signi’¼ücant at lag g we considered its value at
time t + 1 and added the value of the same variables at lag 3,7,11,15,and 19 to
the exogenous variables obtaining the expanded variable
Xt = (xt, xtŌłÆ4, xtŌłÆ8, xtŌłÆ12, xtŌłÆ16, xtŌłÆ20) By considering the exogenous variables
in AR matrix up to order 4 we obtained
lag 1 XtŌłÆ1 = (xtŌłÆ1, xtŌłÆ5, xtŌłÆ9, xtŌłÆ13, xtŌłÆ17, xtŌłÆ21)
lag 2 XtŌłÆ2 = (xtŌłÆ2, xtŌłÆ6, xtŌłÆ10, xtŌłÆ14, xtŌłÆ18, xtŌłÆ22)
lag 3 XtŌłÆ3 = (xtŌłÆ3, xtŌłÆ7, xtŌłÆ11, xtŌłÆ15, xtŌłÆ19, xtŌłÆ23)
lag 4 XtŌłÆ4 = (xtŌłÆ4, xtŌłÆ8, xtŌłÆ12, xtŌłÆ16, xtŌłÆ20, xtŌłÆ24)
In this way is possible to consider all signi’¼ücant lags up to lag g, the lags that
exceed g are not taken into account.
This variables expansion technique is the same used for including the seasonal
lagged values of stocks returns.

We can observe that NDA and JYSK appear to depend only on exogenous
variable, while Danske depends on its past values and on the residual of NDA
of one week before.
From the observed results it appear that the returns of the banking stocks
depend on the past values of the prices of the foreign market indexes,there
also dependencies on Gold and on the currencies USDDKK and EURDKK as
expected.
Moreover all the stocks are in’¼éuenced by the price of Wheat, DANSKE is the
only return which has also an MA component and Jyske Bank return is a’¼Ćected
negatively by Summer which could mean that the bank reduces its activity
during that period.
Residual check
In order to check the adequacy of the model we check if the residual can be white
noise, in order to do that we decide to use the ACF and we plot and investigated
a transformed set of residuals that are uncorrelated given the covariance matrix
of residuals ╬Ż,let ╬ø be the diagonal matrix of its eigenvalus and Q the matrix
of eigenvectors:
╬ŻQ = Q╬ø
==PAGE:54==

Multivariate Analysis
Then
╦£╬Ą = Q╬Ą
The transformed residuals are uncorrelated, for each series of transformed resid-
uals, we can use the control limits of ┬▒ŌłÜ╬╗i to determine if there are large residual
in Figure 3.3 we have the ’¼ürst component of ╦£╬Ą and we can see that the numbers
of residuals that are over the control limits is small compared to the length of
the series from the ACF,in Appendix A.4 we see that the model has just few
signi’¼ücant lags.
Therefore we conclude that the model seems adequate.

3.5
Merging models
In order to have models that includes more variable and therefore,might reduce
the uncertainty of the previous model we decided to merge groups together.
A ’¼ürst we merged Health Care group with Others group then we built model
a marima model on the merged group HC-O, similarly we merged the HC-O
groups with the Financials giving the group F-HC-O and this group with the
Industrial group creating the model F-HC-O-I.
We donŌĆÖt include the Energy group in the merging as it has signi’¼ücantly less
observation than the others.
In order to merge two models we selected just the lags of the exogenous variables
that appeared signi’¼ücant in the models(the output lag), for the AR and the MA
part we took all the signi’¼ücant lags for the stocks and examined just the values
that gives dependency between one group and the other. if X reppresent the
share from the ’¼üst group and Y are the ones from the second group then for
each matrix ╬”h of the AR coe’¼ācients
╬”h

B and C and the elements of matrix A and D that were signi’¼ücant in the previous
models.
Similarly is done for the MA part.
The model is then re estimated and checked for signi’¼ücance in the same way as
it was done for the marima model in the single groups. The resulting equations
for the merged models are:

==PAGE:65==
Chapter 4
Investment Analysis
In this chapter we aimed to build investment strategies, based on the models
built in the previous chapters.
The approach to select the best strategies is the same adopted for the whole
project, starting with simpler models and then evolving to more complex and
realistic ones.Therefore we started from a building a strategy on a single univari-
ate model and then expand it to more univariate models and than multivariate
models.
The strategies are tested and tuned on simulated data generated from the se-
lected models,we assumed that the models captured the behaviour of the time
series. As sometimes the simulated prices, for some of the stocks went below
zero, we adjusted the simulation by imposing a minimum value of 1 DKK for
the prices(changing also the simulated returns values).
The strategies had also been tested with the data from last year(from 23-01-
2017 to 22-01-2018) using models reestimated just on the previous years.
We assumed to start with a portfolio constituted by a certain amount of cash,that
could be used to buy shares of each of the stocks.
For our analysis we mainly started with a budget of 100000 DKK,as increasing
the initial cash amount does not change the gain in percentage,when not con-
sidering transaction fees.
==PAGE:66==
54
Investment Analysis
4.1
Investment Strategies
Given method of modelling a time series and a method of forecasting, many dif-
ferent strategy of investment can be applied, in this elaborate we consider just
a few simple examples to compare univariate and multivariate models,and tech-
niques of forecasting and simulating based on the models, built in the previous
chapters.
4.1.1
Main Investment Strategy
In order to built a strategy we examined the returns rt distribution for each
price series pt, at time t: rt = pt ŌłÆptŌłÆ1. We decided at ’¼ürst to use some per-
centiles(value below which a given percentage of observations in a series fall) of
the returns and one step ahead forecast on simulated data.
Given the series of returns of stock i ri,t, we divided it into a positive and nega-
tive part r+
i,t and rŌłÆ
i,t, then we took the 0th,25th,50th, 75th and 100th percentiles
of the two series, P+ and PŌłÆ, and collected them in a vector qi
qi = (PŌłÆ
0 , PŌłÆ
25, PŌłÆ
50, PŌłÆ
75, PŌłÆ
100, P+
0 , P+
25, P+
50, P+
75, PŌłÆ
100)
We used these vectors, as reference level in the investment strategies to decide
how much we should invest in a certain stock or how many shares of a certain
we should sell if we owned it.
Buying and selling are possible only when there are respectively cash or stocks
in the current portfolio.The number of traded shares is always integer.
If transaction cost are include we considered a ’¼üxed transaction cost of 29
DKK,for orders below 29000 DKK and 1%of the value of transaction for order
above that level,which is the standard transaction fee of the major banks in
Denmark(see Appendix A.8)
First Investment strategy: Given a vector of predicted return of a group of
ns stocks at time t ┬»rt, we divided it into a positive and a negative part ┬»r+
t and
┬»rŌłÆ
t
then we normalized ┬»rt to a 0-1 interval based on the vectors of minimum
(min rt) and the maximum (max rt) historical returns of each stock
╦årt =
┬»rt ŌłÆmin rt
max rt ŌłÆmin rt
The we divided the vector ╦årt in ╦årŌłÆ

4.1 Investment Strategies

The new vector gives the order of selling and buying operations at each time,
’¼ürst we would sell the owned stocks that are predicted to fall, then we would
invest in the stocks that are predicted to rise starting from the one which has
the steepest predicted growth (if there is cash in the current portfolio).
According to this strategy we sell a percentage of the shares of stock i cor-
responding to n1% of the stocks available when the mean of predicted return
is negative and it is lower than the n2 element of the vector of percentiles of
returns qi
n1 = 30 and n2 = PŌłÆ

50,
n1 = 100 and n2 = PŌłÆ
25
We buy stocks corresponding to n1% of the cash available when the mean of
predicted return is positive and it is greater than the n2 element of the qi vector
n1 = 30 and n2 = P+
25,
n1 = 60 and n2 = P+
50,
n1 = 100 and n2 = P+
75

4.1.2
Others Investment Strategies
Some variation of the ’¼üst strategy can be obtained by simply changing the level
of thresholds(how which percentage should be invested when above or below a
value of the percentile vector) or the percentile levels.
The ’¼ürst strategy is quite risk averse, in fact in does not consider selling a stock
even if is predicted to rise in case another one is predicted to rise with a very
steep growth.
Other strategies could be built considering trading in the form of CDF(Contract
For Di’¼Ćerences), that give the investor higher leverage than traditional trad-
ing,anyway we do not explore these strategies in here.
Another investment strategy, that has been implemented is a modi’¼ücation of
the ’¼ürst strategy in order to include the uncertainty on the prediction in the
model,this strategy should give safer investments by reducing the risk,on the
other hand also the gain on average will be smaller.
Given a mean prediction ┬»x and his standard deviation Žā we considered the in-
terval [xL, xU] = ┬»x ┬▒ ŽēŽā and compare ╦£x with the quantiles of historical returns
as in the ’¼ürst strategy.
We used the maximum between xL and 0 when ┬»x is positive and the minimum
between xU and 0 when ┬»x is negative. Žē varies and is the ╬▒ = (0.975, 0.9, 0.8, 0.7, 0.6)
quantile of a normal distribution Žē = F ŌłÆ1(╬▒) = (1.96, 1.282, 0.842, 0.524, 0.253).
The same strategy is also reversed by using xL when ┬»x is negative and xU when ┬»x
is positive, in order to determine if is possible to increase the level of gain,despite
increasing the risk too.
4.2
Simulation and Forecast Methods
In order to compare a strategy on di’¼Ćerent models we decided to use mainly
simulated data.
Using simulated data gives the advantage that is possible to test a strategy
multiple times and have a more accurate estimate of its e’¼Ćectiveness.
On the other hand the problem of simulated data is the assumption that the
models capture accurately the data, and the fact the simulations are always
based on the selected models.
We considered NSim simulations of future data over a period of one trading
year, 261 days,as di’¼Ćerent simulations techniques were adopted the number of
simulation is not the same in every method but it varies,anyway increasing the
number of simulations over a certain number does not a’¼Ćect the average results.
NSim is 100 on the multivariate models that were selected as not relevant and
1000 on the univariate models and the relevant multivariate models.
==PAGE:70==
58
Investment Analysis
The two measures we used in order to compare methods are the gain and the
fail rate
gain of simulation i = gi = initial investment-value of ’¼ünal portfolio
initial investment
failrate = fr =
PNSim
i=1 (gi1(gi<0))
NSim
We decided to use the mean ,the median and 75th percentile of gain to compare
di’¼Ćerent simulations strategies because the mean is very in’¼éuenced by outliers
and the median can be too low compared to the mean, so we wanted to have
also a statistic that take into account that there are simulation with very high
average gain, but is closer to the value of the majority of simulations.

4.2.2
Fist Multivariate Method
In order to avoid jumps in the simulation we started the simulation 100 days
before the end of the series(22-01-2018),we therefore had an overlapping period
between old data and new simulated data. For the multivariate case, we instead
tried four di’¼Ćerent method for generating the simulated time series and fore-
casting future values, based on a marima model.
The variables in the marima models can be divided in two categories "ran-
dom" R and "not random" N this two groups are respectively constituted by
the share returns and the remaining variables(exogenous variables,long lagged
share returns and lagged exogenous variables),the AR and MA coe’¼ācient of the
non random variables had been set to zero when doing the marima modelling,as
we are only interested in their e’¼Ćect on the share prices and not the opposite.
The methods we used consisted in simulating the data using samples from a
standard normal distribution scaled using the covariance matrix of the residuals
of the marima model.
If the marima model has k variables and the length of the simulation is nT where
nT = nF + nOV with nF and nOV respectively the number of future data(261)
and the length of the overlapping period(100) then we considered a matrix U of
random samples from normal standard distribution of size nT ├Ś k.
Given a matrix B of dimension k ├Ś k
B = V SV T
(4.7)

expected gain, this method is not adequate for simulations.

4.2.4
Others Multivariate Methods
In order to improve the forecasted value of the series and the simulations we de-
cided to try to simulate the value of the exogenous variables, based on univariate
ARIMA models of the predictors,built when doing the prewhitening process.
The value of the dummies variable has been set to the value of the previous
year, also for those variables that does not follow an ARIMA model, Tempera-
ture,GDP ,INR has been set to their values of one year before.
The main problem of this method his that simulating from the ARIMA, models
introduces more uncertainty, giving the high volatility-high gain problem similar
to the one observed when applying the second simulation method.
The expected gains on the simulated data is too high and not realistic as it is
shown by the ’¼ügure in Appendix A.6.2.
Results from this method are include in AppendixA.7 listing A.9 where the gain
and fail rate results are shown, they are identi’¼üed by the string SIM3X_M
We decided, in the end, to simply use the data from the last year as in the
univariate simulation and forecast method.

and more over they are inside the maximum and minimum range of historical
data shown by Figure 4.6, which display the Prediction and simulation of stocks

Including uncertainty and compare with real
data
In order to measure the reliability of the data we compare the result of strategy
1 when forecasting with a marima model on simulated data,with the results of
the same strategy when forecasting on real data.
==PAGE:80==

In order to do so we re estimated the coe’¼ācients of marima models removing
the last year of data, then we forecasted using one step ahead forecast,with the
last multivariate technique, the results of this method are listed in Appendix

data.

instead of the mean prediction a greater or a lower value inside the con’¼üdence
interval for prediction, the results of this strategy are listed in Appendix A.11
and the graphs are shown in Appendix. From the results we can see that in gen-
eral choosing values that are closer to the maximum of the con’¼üdence interval
give more gain, without increasing the fail rate in general.
This strategy has been tested only on multivariate model excluding the model
on F-HC-O, but it has not been explored fully.
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 5
Conclusion
With this elaborate we explored univariate and multivariate models for time
series.
The focus of our analysis has been on the Close prices of the stocks in the C20
index and their return.
We tried to use models and strategies that are quite complex and not often used
in the ’¼ünancial world and in order to built them we started with simple model
and building the next model based on the result from the previous one, this
procedure is quite slow but it allows a better and deep understanding of the
argument.
The models need further exploration but from the collected results, it seems
that multivariate model in general outperform the univariate one, giving results
that are more reliable and with less uncertainty.
it also seem that using more small marima model could be better than using
merged ones.

70
Conclusion

# P-value
calculations
==PAGE:166==
154

# X=NULL
result=F
correction1
<- function(pvalues) {
max_p <- max(pvalues)
if ( correction
== "None") {
result
<- ifelse(max_p < alpha , TRUE , FALSE)
}
else if ( correction
== "Bonf" | correction
== "Bonferroni") {
result
<- ifelse(max_p < alpha/length(pvalues),
TRUE , FALSE)
}
else if ( correction
== "holm") {
pi <- sort(pvalues)
for (n in 1: length(pi)) {
r <- ifelse(pi[n] < alpha/(length(pvalues)-n+1) , T,F)
if(!r)
break
}
result
<- r
}
else
result
<- NULL
return(result)
}

armacoef=function(modelcoef ,order){
phi=paste("=1+",paste(-round(modelcoef [1: order [1]] ,3) ,"\bm{B}^{}",collapse=
"+",sep=""),sep="")
theta=paste("=1+",paste(round(modelcoef [( order [1]+1) :( sum(order [1:2]))],3),
"\bm{B}^{}",collapse="+",sep=""),sep="")
Phi=paste("=1+",paste(round(-modelcoef [(sum(order [1:2]) +1) :( sum(order [1:2])
+order [3]) ],3),"\bm{B}^{}",collapse="+",sep=""),sep="")
Theta=paste("=1+",paste(round(modelcoef [(sum(order [1:2])+order [3]+1) :( sum(
order [1:2])+sum(order [3:4]))],3),"\bm{B}^{}",collapse="+",sep=""),sep=
"")
return(list(phi ,theta ,Phi ,Theta))
}

correction1
<- function(pvalues ,correction ,alpha) {
if(missing(alpha))
alpha =0.05
max_p <- max(pvalues)
if ( correction
== "None") {
result
<- ifelse(max_p < alpha , TRUE , FALSE)
}
else if ( correction
== "Bonf" | correction
== "Bonferroni") {
result
<- ifelse(max_p < alpha/length(pvalues),
TRUE , FALSE)
}
else if ( correction
== "holm") {
pi <- sort(pvalues)
for (n in 1: length(pi)) {
r <- ifelse(pi[n] < alpha/(length(pvalues)-n+1) , T,F)
if(!r)
break
}
result
<- r
}
else
result
<- NULL
return(result)
}

simulateexo =function (exomodels=NULL ,exoestim=F) {
# browser ()
ntot=NF+nstart
exo.arma=exomodels$exo.arma
R=exomodels$R
DF=exomodels$DF
X_d=diff(as.matrix(DF[,-1]))
exo.arma=exo.arma [ -14]
nx=length(exo.arma)
Xsim=matrix (0,nrow=ntot ,ncol =18)
colnames(Xsim)=colnames(DF[,-1])
colnames(Xsim)[14]="Temp"
# d=tail(DF[,c(1 ,9 ,10 ,15:19)],ntot -nsim)
R=rbind(tail(DF[,c(1 ,9 ,10 ,15:19)],ntot -NF),R)
Xsim[,c(8 ,9 ,14:18) ]=as.matrix(R[1:ntot , -1])
for(f in 1:nx){
if(!(f %in% c(8 ,9))){
Xsim[,f]= tail(DF[,f+1], ntot)
if(exoestim){
mod=exo.arma [[f]]
xd=X_d[,f]
model
<- Arima(y=xd , order=c(mod$arma [1],0, mod$arma [2]) , seasonal=
list(order=c(mod$arma [3],0,mod$arma [4]) ,period =5) ,
include.mean = "intercept"%in%names(mod$coef),
transform.pars = F, method = "ML",fixed=mod$coef)
sim=simulate(model ,xreg=NULL , future = T)
Xsim[,f]=as.numeric(sim [1: ntot ])+tail(DF[,f+1] ,1)
}
}
}
# browser ()
Xsim=Xsim[,colnames(Xsim)%in% colnames( extralagmat )]
Xsim=t(Xsim)
return(Xsim)
}

##ŌĆÖ title
forec.var
##ŌĆÖ
##ŌĆÖ description
Function
for
calculation
of
variances
of nstep
forecasts
##ŌĆÖ using a marima
type
model.
##ŌĆÖ
##ŌĆÖ param
marima
marima
object (cov.u and ar.estimates
and
##ŌĆÖ
ma.estimates
are

##ŌĆÖ param

##ŌĆÖ
##ŌĆÖ param

##ŌĆÖ

##ŌĆÖ

when

##ŌĆÖ

##ŌĆÖ
##ŌĆÖ return

= variance - covariances
for
nstep

##ŌĆÖ (an array
with

##ŌĆÖ
##ŌĆÖ return

= corresponding

##ŌĆÖ of the

##ŌĆÖ
forec.var
<- function(marima , nstep = 1) {
# cat(" Calculation
of
forecasting
variances.
\n")
sig2
<- marima$resid.cov
ar.poly
<- marima$ar.estimates
ma.poly
<- marima$ma.estimates
d <- dim(sig2)
#
# if(is.null(dif.poly)) {
#
dif.poly
<- diag(d[1])
# }
#
# dif.poly
<- check.one(dif.poly)
#
kvar = dim(sig2)[1]
# ar.poly <-pol.mul(ar.poly , dif.poly , L=nstep +1)
if (is.null(ma.poly)) {
ma.poly
<- diag(kvar)
}
ar.poly
<- check.one(ar.poly)
ma.poly
<- check.one(ma.poly)
if (nstep < 1) {
nstep
<- 1
}
xsi.poly
<- rand.shock(ar.poly , ma.poly , nstep)
var
<- xsi.poly
# cat(ŌĆÖnsteps =ŌĆÖ,1:nstep ,ŌĆÖ\nŌĆÖ)
for (i in 1: nstep) {
# cat(ŌĆÖi=ŌĆÖ,i,ŌĆÖ\nŌĆÖ)
var[, , i] <- matrix(xsi.poly[, , i], nrow = kvar) %*%
sig2 %*% t(matrix(xsi.poly[, , i], nrow = kvar))
if (i > 1) {
==PAGE:211==
A.9 R code
199
var[, , i] <- var[, , i] + var[, , (i - 1)]
}
}
d <- dim(var)[3] - 1
# cat(ŌĆÖd=ŌĆÖ,d,ŌĆÖ\nŌĆÖ)
# cat(ŌĆÖxsi.poly =ŌĆÖ,1:nstep ,ŌĆÖ\nŌĆÖ)
# print(round(xsi.poly ,4))
# cat(ŌĆÖvar=ŌĆÖ,ŌĆÖ\nŌĆÖ) print(round(var[,,1:d],4))
return(list(pred.var = var[, , 1:d], rand.shock = xsi.poly[, , 1:d]))
}

scaleyP =(yP -Q[ ,1])/(Q[,10]-Q[ ,1])
yPp=yP
scaleyPp=scaleyP
yPp=YSM[nf ,,sm]
scaleyPp =(yPp -Q[ ,1])/(Q[,10]-Q[ ,1])
buysellorder =c(yP[yP <=0][ order(scaleyP[yP <=0] , decreasing = F)],yP[yP
>0][ order(scaleyP[yP >0], decreasing = T)])
buysellorderp =c(yPp[yPp <=0][ order(scaleyPp[yPp <=0] , decreasing = F)],
yPp[yPp >0][ order(scaleyPp[yPp >0], decreasing = T)])
buyname=names( buysellorder )
buynamep=names( buysellorderp )
if(nf >1){
Budget[sm ,nf]= Budget[sm ,nf -1]
Stock[nf ,,sm]= Stock[nf -1,,sm]
Budgetp[sm ,nf]= Budgetp[sm ,nf -1]
Stockp[nf ,,sm]= Stockp[nf -1,,sm]
Budgetw[sm ,nf]= Budgetw[sm ,nf -1]
Stockw[nf ,,sm]= Stockw[nf -1,,sm]
}
portval[sm ,nf]= Budget[sm ,nf]+sum(PSM[nf ,,sm]*Stock[nf ,,sm])
portvalp[sm ,nf]= Budgetp[sm ,nf]+ sum(PSM[nf ,,sm]*Stockp[nf ,,sm])
portvalw[sm ,nf]= Budgetw[sm ,nf]+ sum(PSM[nf ,,sm]*Stockw[nf ,,sm])
pweigths[nf ,,sm]=c(Budget[sm ,nf],PSM[nf ,,sm]*Stock[nf ,,sm])/portval[
sm ,nf]
pweigthsp[nf ,,sm]=c(Budgetp[sm ,nf],PSM[nf ,,sm]*Stockp[nf ,,sm])/
portvalp[sm ,nf]
# pweights.melt
<- reshape2 :: melt(pweigths)
# pweightsp.melt
<- reshape2 :: melt(pweigthsp)
if(track){
cat("\n_________________________________________________________ŌÉŻ\n
ŌÉŻSIM",sm ,"ŌÉŻTime",paste(nf ,"/",NF ,sep=""),
"\n_________________________________________________________ŌÉŻ\n

m_b_format = function(x) {
# browser ()
b.index = x >= 1e9
m.index = x >= 1e6 & x < 1e9
k.index = x >= 1e3 & x < 1e6
no.index= x < 1e3
output = formatC(x, format = "d", big.mark = ",")
output[b.index] = paste(formatC(x[b.index] / 1e9 , digits = 2, format = "f
"), "B")
output[m.index] = paste(formatC(x[m.index] / 1e6 , digits = 2, format = "f
"), "M")
output[k.index] = paste(formatC(x[k.index] / 1e3 , digits = 2, format = "f
"), "K")
output[no.index] =round(x,2)
return(output)
}
